        <title>Genome draft of the Arabidopsis relative Pachycladon cheesemanii reveals novel strategies to tolerate New Zealandâ€™s high ultraviolet B radiation environment | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Pachycladon cheesemanii is a close relative of Arabidopsis thaliana and is an allotetraploid perennial herb which is widespread in the South Island of New Zealand. It grows at altitudes of up to 1000&#8201;m where it is subject to relatively high levels of ultraviolet (UV)-B radiation. To gain first insights into how Pachycladon copes with UV-B stress, we sequenced its genome and compared the UV-B tolerance of two Pachycladon accessions with those of two A. thaliana accessions from different altitudes. A high-quality draft genome of P. cheesemanii was assembled with a high percentage of conserved single-copy plant orthologs. Synteny analysis with genomes from other species of the Brassicaceae family found a close phylogenetic relationship of P. cheesemanii with Boechera stricta from Brassicaceae lineage I. While UV-B radiation caused a greater growth reduction in the A. thaliana accessions than in the P. cheesemanii accessions, growth was not reduced in one P. cheesemanii accession. The homologues of A. thaliana UV-B radiation response genes were duplicated in P. cheesemanii, and an expression analysis of those genes indicated that the tolerance mechanism in P. cheesemanii appears to differ from that in A. thaliana. Although the P. cheesemanii genome shows close similarity with that of A. thaliana, it appears to have evolved novel strategies allowing the plant to tolerate relatively high UV-B radiation."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Pachycladon cheesemanii is a close relative of Arabidopsis thaliana and is an allotetraploid perennial herb which is widespread in the South Island of New Zealand. It grows at altitudes of up to 1000&#8201;m where it is subject to relatively high levels of ultraviolet (UV)-B radiation. <script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome draft of the Arabidopsis relative Pachycladon cheesemanii reveals novel strategies to tolerate New Zealandâ€™s high ultraviolet B radiation environment"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Pachycladon cheesemanii is a close relative of Arabidopsis thaliana and is an allotetraploid perennial herb which is widespread in the South Island of New Zealand. It grows at altitudes of up to 1000â€‰m where it is subject to relatively high levels of ultraviolet (UV)-B radiation. To gain first insights into how Pachycladon copes with UV-B stress, we sequenced its genome and compared the UV-B tolerance of two Pachycladon accessions with those of two A. thaliana accessions from different altitudes. A high-quality draft genome of P. cheesemanii was assembled with a high percentage of conserved single-copy plant orthologs. Synteny analysis with genomes from other species of the Brassicaceae family found a close phylogenetic relationship of P. cheesemanii with Boechera stricta from Brassicaceae lineage I. While UV-B radiation caused a greater growth reduction in the A. thaliana accessions than in the P. cheesemanii accessions, growth was not reduced in one P. cheesemanii accession. The homologues of A. thaliana UV-B radiation response genes were duplicated in P. cheesemanii, and an expression analysis of those genes indicated that the tolerance mechanism in P. cheesemanii appears to differ from that in A. thaliana. Although the P. cheesemanii genome shows close similarity with that of A. thaliana, it appears to have evolved novel strategies allowing the plant to tolerate relatively high UV-B radiation."/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Pachycladon cheesemanii</i> is a close relative of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and is an allotetraploid perennial herb which is widespread in the South Island of New Zealand. It grows at altitudes of up to 1000â€‰m where it is subject to relatively high levels of ultraviolet (UV)-B radiation. To gain first insights into how <i>Pachycladon</i> copes with UV-B stress, we sequenced its genome and compared the UV-B tolerance of two <i>Pachycladon</i> accessions with those of two <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions from different altitudes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>A high-quality draft genome of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> was assembled with a high percentage of conserved single-copy plant orthologs. Synteny analysis with genomes from other species of the Brassicaceae family found a close phylogenetic relationship of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> with <i>Boechera stricta</i> from Brassicaceae lineage I. While UV-B radiation caused a greater growth reduction in the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions than in the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions, growth was not reduced in one <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accession. The homologues of <i>A. thaliana</i> UV-B radiation response genes were duplicated in <i>P. cheesemanii</i>, and an expression analysis of those genes indicated that the tolerance mechanism in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> appears to differ from that in <i>A. thaliana</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusion</h3><p>Although the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome shows close similarity with that of <i>A. thaliana</i>, it appears to have evolved novel strategies allowing the plant to tolerate relatively high UV-B radiation.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Pachycladon</i> is an allopolyploid genus of the Brassicaceae family with eight perennial species endemic to the South Island of New Zealand and one species to Tasmania (Australia). These <i>Pachycladon</i> species are believed to have originated around 1â€“3.5 million years ago in New Zealand and are primarily distributed across the alpine regions of the South Island [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Heenan P, Mitchell A. Phylogeny, biogeography and adaptive radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in the mountains of South Island, New Zealand. J Biogeogr. 2003;30:1737â€“49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1171">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yogeeswaran K, Voelckel C, Joly S, Heenan P. Pachycladon. In: Kole C, editor. Wild crop relatives: genomic and breeding resources wild relatives of oilseeds. Tokyo: Springer; 2011. p. 227â€“49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1174">2</a>]. <i>Pachycladon cheesemanii</i> is the most widespread of the <i>Pachycladon</i> species with a broad longitudinal distribution in New Zealand and a wide altitudinal range from 10â€‰m to 1600â€‰m above sea level [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Heenan P, Mitchell A. Phylogeny, biogeography and adaptive radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in the mountains of South Island, New Zealand. J Biogeogr. 2003;30:1737â€“49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1184">1</a>].</p><p><i>Pachycladon</i>â€™s allopolyploid genome (2<i>n</i>â€‰=â€‰20) consists of two subgenomes which resulted from intra- or interspecific crossing [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Song Q, Chen ZJ. Epigenetic and developmental regulation in plant polyploids. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2015;24:101â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1195">3</a>]. Karyotype comparisons between extant <i>Pachycladon</i> species and the theoretical Ancestral Crucifer Karyotype showed that the chromosome structure had undergone multiple rearrangements prior to the allopolyploidy event taking place [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Mandakova T, Heenan PB, Lysak MA. Island species radiation and karyotypic stasis in Pachycladon allopolyploids. BMC Evol Biol. 2010;10:367." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1201">4</a>], and this has hampered efforts to trace back <i>Pachycladon</i>â€™s progenitors. Phylogenetic analysis of <i>Pachycladon</i> species based on five single-copy nuclear genes indicated that one of the genome copies was derived from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage, while another was similar to both <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Brassica</i> lineages [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1220">5</a>]. However, a comparison of 547 homeologous gene pairs from <i>P. cheesemanii</i> and <i>P. fastigiatum</i> with the homologous genes from <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> found that no set of genes showed significantly different identity to <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> homologues, suggesting the two <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenomes are derived from the same lineage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Gruenheit N, Deusch O, Esser C, Becker M, Voelckel C, Lockhart P. Cutoffs and k-mers: implications from a transcriptome study in allopolyploid plants. BMC Genomics. 2012;13:92." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1246">6</a>]. Data from analysis of the nuclear gene <i>CHALCONE SYNTHASE</i> (<i>CHS</i>) further supported the idea that both <i>Pachycladon</i> genome copies stem from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zhao B, Liu L, Tan D, Wang J. Analysis of phylogenetic relationships of Brassicaceae species based on Chs sequences. Biochem Syst Ecol. 2010;38:731â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1261">7</a>].</p><p>Polyploidization has been suggested to contribute to plantsâ€™ evolution and environmental adaptation under selection pressure [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowers JE, Chapman BA, Rong J, Paterson AH. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature. 2003;422:433â€“8." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1267">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Brochmann C, Brysting A, Alsos I, Borgen L, Grundt H, Scheen A-C, Elven R. Polyploidy in arctic plants. Biol J Linn Soc. 2004;82:521â€“36." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1267_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wood TE, Takebayashi N, Barker MS, Mayrose I, Greenspoon PB, Rieseberg LH. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. P Natl Acad Sci USA. 2009;106:13875â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1270">10</a>]. Plants with polyploid genomes can benefit from functional diversification of redundant gene copies, with one gene copy retaining the original function, guaranteeing the plantâ€™s regular growth and development, while the other can evolve to confer novel phenotypes, such as protection against challenging environmental conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Comai L. The advantages and disadvantages of being polyploid. Nat Rev Genet. 2005;6:836â€“46." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1273">11</a>]. Thus, higher levels of UV radiation in New Zealand compared with locations in the Northern Hemisphere at similar latitudes may have contributed to the evolution of the <i>Pachycladon</i> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="McKenzie R, Liley B, Kotkamp M, Shiona H, Lopez L. Long term changes in UV in New Zealand due to ozone depletion and other causes. Auckland: NIWA UV Workshop; 2014. p. 15â€“7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1279">12</a>].</p><p>UV radiation is classified into three types, UV-A, UV-B and UV-C. While UV-C does not penetrate the atmosphere, some UV-B radiation reaches Earthâ€™s surface, where it can damage important molecules like DNA. In order to acclimate to UV-B radiation, plants have developed multiple strategies, including reducing leaf area by curling of the leaves, inhibiting leaf and plant growth [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Nascimento LBS, Moreira NS, Leal-Costa MV, Costa SS, Tavares ES. Induction of wound-periderm-like tissue in Kalanchoe pinnata (lam.) Pers.(Crassulaceae) leaves as a defence response to high UV-B radiation levels. Ann Bot-London. 2015;116:763â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1285">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Qaderi MM, Yeung EC, Reid DM. Growth and physiological responses of an invasive alien species, Silene noctiflora, during two developmental stages to four levels of ultraviolet-B radiation. Ecoscience. 2008;15:150â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1288">14</a>] and increasing light reflection by inducing the production of a cuticular wax layer and the biosynthesis of light-absorbing secondary metabolites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="SteinmÃ¼ller D, Tevini M. Action of ultraviolet radiation (UV-B) upon cuticular waxes in some crop plants. Planta. 1985;164:557â€“64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1291">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lavola A, Julkunen-Tiitto R, Aphalo P, Rosa TDL, Lehto T. The effect of u.v.-B radiation on u.v.-absorbing secondary metabolites in birch seedlings grown under simulated Forest soil conditions. New Phytol. 2010;137:617â€“21." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1294">16</a>]. Nevertheless, excess UV-B radiation can cause the development of hypersensitive response-like necrotic lesions and plant death [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jenkins GI. The UV-B photoreceptor UVR8: from structure to physiology. Plant Cell. 2014;26:21â€“37." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1297">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kalbina I, Strid Ã…. Supplementary ultraviolet-B irradiation reveals differences in stress responses between Arabidopsis thaliana ecotypes. Plant Cell Environ. 2006;29:754â€“63." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1297_1">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hectors K, Prinsen E, De Coen W, Jansen MA, Guisez Y. Arabidopsis thaliana plants acclimated to low dose rates of ultraviolet B radiation show specific changes in morphology and gene expression in the absence of stress symptoms. New Phytol. 2007;175:255â€“70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1301">19</a>].</p><p>UV-B radiation is perceived by the UVB-resistance 8 (UVR8) photoreceptor which was discovered by the UV-B hypersensitivity of the <i>uvr8</i> mutant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kliebenstein DJ, Lim JE, Landry LG, Last RL. Arabidopsis UVR8 regulates ultraviolet-B signal transduction and tolerance and contains sequence similarity to human regulator of chromatin condensation 1. Plant Physiol. 2002;130:234â€“43." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1311">20</a>]. The crystal structure of the UVR8 protein showed that its core domain consists of a covalently bound homodimer [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wu D, Hu Q, Yan Z, Chen W, Yan C, Huang X, Zhang J, Yang P, Deng H, Wang J. Structural basis of ultraviolet-B perception by UVR8. Nature. 2012;484:214â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1314">21</a>]. After UV-B radiation, this homodimer dissociates and monomeric UVR8 interacts with CONSTITUTIVE PHOTOMORPHOGENIC 1 (COP1) and transcription factors including ELONGATED HYPOCOTYL 5 (HY5) and HY5-HOMOLOG (HYH) to induce the expression of UV-B-responsive genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Heijde M, Ulm R. UV-B photoreceptor-mediated signalling in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 2012;17:230â€“7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1317">22</a>]. Induced genes included those that encode CHS, FLAVANONE 3-HYDROXYLASE (F3H) and FLAVONOL SYNTHASE 1 (FLS1), which are core enzymes involved in the biosynthesis of flavonoids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Di Ferdinando M, Brunetti C, Fini A, Tattini M. Flavonoids as antioxidants in plants under abiotic stresses. In: Ahmad P, Prasad MNV, editors. Abiotic stress responses in plants. New York: Springer; 2012. p. 159â€“79." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1320">23</a>] and are believed to function as a UV-absorbing sun screen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Day TA. Relating UV-B radiation screening effectiveness of foliage to absorbing-compound concentration and anatomical characteristics in a diverse group of plants. Oecologia. 1993;95:542â€“50." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1324">24</a>]. Other induced genes include <i>PHOTOLYASE 1</i> (<i>PHR1</i>), which encodes protein phosphate starvation response 1, and <i>EARLY LIGHT-INDUCIBLE PROTEIN1</i> (<i>ELIP1</i>). ELIP1 plays a role in the interaction of UV-B-induced monomeric UVR8 with chromatin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Cloix C, Jenkins GI. Interaction of the Arabidopsis UV-B-specific signaling component UVR8 with chromatin. Mol Plant. 2008;1:118â€“28." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1339">25</a>]. It was found that the UVR8-dependent pathway responds to a wide range of UV-B radiation (0.1â€“12â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup>). Another less well-understood UV-response pathway was found that functions independently of UVR8. By treating <i>uvr8</i> mutants with relatively high UV-B radiation levels (1â€“12â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup>), several genes induced by this pathway were identified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Brown BA, Jenkins GI. UV-B signaling pathways with different fluence-rate response profiles are distinguished in mature Arabidopsis leaf tissue by requirement for UVR8, HY5, and HYH. Plant Physiol. 2008;146:576â€“88." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1354">26</a>].</p><p>Since <i>P. cheesemanii</i> survives in New Zealand's high UV-B radiation environments, this species may have evolved distinct UV-B-radiation response pathways. To learn how this species is able to cope in its unique environment, we first assembled a high-quality draft genome of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> and attempted to reveal the two highly similar subgenomes. The draft genome was used to identify <i>P. cheesemanii</i> candidate genes likely involved in UV-B radiation response pathways. However, interestingly, the UV-B-induced expression pattern of these genes differed from that observed in two <i>A. thaliana</i> accession with differing UV-B responses, suggesting that a distinct UV-B radiation response pathway has evolved in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> to enable adaptation to the high UV-B radiation environment in New Zealand.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and assessment</h3><p>We extracted <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston genomic DNA for whole genome sequencing. The Illumina sequencing technology was used to obtain high coverage sequence reads to help us determine its ancestry and current gene-set. Paired-end and mate-pair libraries were sequenced and ~â€‰56 Gb of DNA sequence obtained. Raw reads (483,792,966 reads) were subsequently trimmed using the cutadapt algorithm that is present in the trim_galore package. Using k-mer analysis (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM1">1</a>) the genome size was estimated to encompass 596â€‰Mb. Multiple aligners (Platanus and SOAPdenovo) with different k-mer lengths were used to generate genome assemblies. Subsequently, these assemblies were further evaluated using multiple metrics, and the best one (51-k-mer assembly) was selected based on the assembly size and N50 from Platanus (P.k51) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM2">2</a>). The assemblies using SOAP resulted in a higher scaffold size compared to Platanus, but also a much higher number of gaps and lower percentage of complete single copy orthologues. Therefore, Platanus was used as the preferred genome assembler. The total assembly size using P.k51 was ~â€‰422â€‰Mb and this represented 70.8% of the estimated genome size. The longest scaffold was 418â€‰kb, while the number of scaffolds of lengthâ€‰â‰¥â€‰500 andâ€‰â‰¥â€‰1000 bases were 53,782 and 23,900, spanning ~â€‰300â€‰Mb and ~â€‰280â€‰Mb of assembly size, respectively. The N50 for the assembly (scaffolds â‰¥500â€‰bp) was 24,761 bases (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Tab1">1</a>). This result indicated that the assembled genome draft was highly fragmented.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A high amount of repetitive DNA in the genome could be one reason for the fragmented genome assembly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Schatz MC, Witkowski J, McCombie WR. Current challenges in de novo plant genome sequencing and assembly. Genome Biol. 2012;13:243." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1460">27</a>]. Therefore, the repeat content in the genome draft was analyzed using different repeat identification tools, and it was estimated that ~â€‰43% of the total assembly size comprised repeat regions (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM3">3</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM4">4</a>). Among these, 15.96% were annotated as â€œretrotransposonsâ€, 6.84% as â€œDNA transposonsâ€ and 19.89% as â€œunclassified repeatsâ€.</p><p>BUSCO assessment revealed that 96.2% highly conserved plant orthologs were â€œcompleteâ€, 1.5% â€œfragmentedâ€ and 2.3% â€œmissingâ€. Reads were mapped back to the assembly using Bowtie 2 to show 96.98% alignment (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Tab2">2</a>). The <i>P. cheesemanii</i> leaf transcriptome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Gruenheit N, Deusch O, Esser C, Becker M, Voelckel C, Lockhart P. Cutoffs and k-mers: implications from a transcriptome study in allopolyploid plants. BMC Genomics. 2012;13:92." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1478">6</a>] was aligned against the assembled genome using PASA, and 97.94% of transcripts could be mapped to the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Tab2">2</a>). A total of 47,821 protein coding genes were predicted using MAKER, with an average transcript size of 1544â€‰bp and 4.42 exons per gene. With regard to non-coding RNAs, 115 rRNA, 707 tRNA and 209 miRNA genes were predicted. In addition, in a comparison of the alleles in <i>P. cheesemanii,</i> 434,467 SNPs and 123,778 SSRs were identified, highlighting the highly polymorphic information content of its genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM5">5</a>). Thus, the results showed a fragmented genome draft, which may be the result of the high number of repeat elements in non-coding regions or/and having two highly similar genomes to contend with. Nevertheless, the assembly of coding regions was deemed of high quality, based on BUSCO and PASA analyses.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Assessment statistics of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome functional annotation</h3><p>Each of the predicted genes was functionally annotated by using BLASTX against National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) non-redundant protein [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Pruitt KD, Tatusova T, Maglott DR. NCBI reference sequences (RefSeq): a curated non-redundant sequence database of genomes, transcripts and proteins. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006;35(Database issue):D61â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1532">28</a>] and Uniprot databases for green plants (Viridiplantae) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Tab3">3</a>). About 84% of the predicted genes had a blast hit against either NCBI nr or Uniprot databases, or against both. Among these, 63% had a hit in the manually curated Swissprot database. Based on the BLASTX result against NCBI nr, the highest number of hits was with <i>Camelina sativa</i> (24.4%), followed by <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i> (22.7%), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (19.0%) and <i>Capsella rubella</i> (17.3%), all belonging to the Brassicaceae family [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1551">29</a>]. InterProScan identified protein signatures for 89.81% of the predicted proteins, and 2597 genes were classified as transcription factor (TF) encoding genes. Similar to <i>A. thaliana</i>, bHLH (239), MYB (212), ERF (211) and NAC (179) TFs comprised the largest TF families in <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. The predicted genes were used for classification into pathways using the KEGG database. Similar to other plant species, the terms â€œmetabolic pathwaysâ€ and â€œbiosynthesis of secondary metabolitesâ€ were assigned to the largest numbers of the predicted genes in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (2930 and 1594, respectively) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM6">6</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Annotation statistics of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Synteny analysis of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome draft within Brassicaceae species</h3><p>It has been reported that the two <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenomes originate from the hybridization of two species of the Brassicaceae family, one each from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Brassica</i> lineages [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1641">5</a>]. Here, the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome was aligned against all publicly available Brassicaceae genomes using MUMmer to perform synteny analysis. Of 28 available Brassicaceae genomes, seven each were from the Brassiceae and Camelineae tribes, four from the Eutremeae tribe, three from the Arabideae tribe, two from the Cardamineae tribe, and one each from the Thlaspideae, Sisymbrieae, Euclidieae, Boechereae, and Aethionemeae tribes (the tribes of Brassicaceae Lineage I: Camelineae, Cardamineae, and Boechereae; the tribes of Lineage II: Sisymbrieae and Brassiceae; the tribe of Lineage III: Euclidieae; the tribes of Expanded Lineage II (EII): Thlaspideae and Eutremeae; the tribe of the basal lineage: Aethionemeae; the unassigned tribe: Arabideae) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1648">29</a>]. <i>Tarenaya hassleriana</i> from the Cleomaceae family was selected as an outgroup [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1654">29</a>]. Species with the highest alignment percentage (Maximal Unique Matches: MUMs) against the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome belong to Boechereae (29%), Camelineae (~â€‰20%) and Eutremeae (~â€‰15%). All pairwise combinations of the Brassicaceae genomes were used to estimate the cumulative alignment percentage with the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome to determine possible ancestral genomes of <i>Pachycladon</i>. The combination of <i>Boechera stricta</i> and <i>Eutrema heterophyllum</i> had the highest cumulative alignment with <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (37.35%) at the genome level (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig1">1</a>a).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="763" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Prediction of the origin of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome. <b>a</b> MUMmer alignment percentage (MUMs: Maximal Unique Matches) of <i>Pachycladon</i> against other sequenced Brassicaceae genomes. The numbers indicates cumulative percentage of MUMs for the respective pair of species against <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. <b>b</b> OrthoFinder output showing orthologous clusters between <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (pch), <i>A. thaliana</i> (ath), <i>B. stricta</i> (bst), <i>E. heterophyllum</i> (ehe) and <i>C. sativa</i> (csa). <b>c</b> Dendrogram of five species with high scores in MUMmer alignment. Numbers represent branch lengths</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>From the species with the highest alignment percentage against the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome, three species from Brassicaceae Lineage I (<i>C. sativa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>B. stricta</i>, two from the Camelineae tribe, and one from the Boechereae tribe) and one from Lineage EII (<i>E. heterophyllum</i>, from Eutremeae tribe) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1740">29</a>] were selected for protein ortholog analysis. To identify orthologs, predicted proteins of all five species were blasted against each other in a pairwise manner for a total of 25 combinations. The BLAST searches were further processed using OrthoFinder to identify orthologs. A total of 182,585 genes (76%) were assigned to 20,553 orthogroups that included 14,971 orthogroups shared within the five species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig1">1</a>b). For <i>P. cheesemanii</i>, 66.4% of the genes (31,749) were assigned to 87% (17,881) of the total orthogroups. Among these orthogroups, 15 novel orthogroups containing 72 genes were present in <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. Based on the orthogroups, a dendrogram of the five species was constructed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig1">1</a>c). In accordance with the synteny analysis, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> showed the closest relationship with <i>B. stricta</i>, followed by <i>C. sativa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. Beside the orthogroups that were shared by all species, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> shared the highest number of orthogroups with <i>C. sativa</i> (2191), followed by <i>B. stricta</i> (1753), <i>A. thaliana</i> (1721) and <i>E. heterophyllum</i> (923). Thus, the data suggests that <i>P. cheesemanii</i> has a closer phylogenetic relationship with species from Lineage I of the Brassicaceae family than to those of Lineage EII.</p><p>Next, we used the <i>P. cheesemanii</i>, <i>B. stricta</i>, <i>E. heterophyllum</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes to analyze the GO enrichment patterns to further study the phylogenetic relationships of these species. The predicted gene annotations encompassed all major GO terms, suggesting that a core GO term set is present in the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome annotation (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig2">2</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM7">7</a>). A comparison with the GO enrichment distributions of <i>B. stricta</i>, <i>E. heterophyllum</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> revealed a similar pattern across all three GO categories in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> and <i>B. stricta</i>, while the <i>E. heterophyllum</i> pattern was considerably different from the other three species of Brassicaceae Lineage I (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig2">2</a>). Therefore, this result provides further support for the closer evolutionary grouping of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> with <i>B. stricta</i> of Brassicaceae Lineage I, than to <i>E. heterophyllum</i> of Lineage EII.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="790" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene Ontology (GO) annotation. Comparison of GO terms between <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (pch), <i>A. thaliana</i> (ath), <i>B. stricta</i> (bst) and <i>E. heterophyllum</i> (ehe)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Different UV-B responses in <i>Pachycladon cheesemanii</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>
</h3><p>The New Zealand environment is prone to high UV-B radiation levels naturally [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="McKenzie R, Connor B, Bodeker G. Increased summertime UV radiation in New Zealand in response to ozone loss. Science. 1999;285:1709â€“11." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1879">30</a>]. We therefore hypothesized that <i>P. cheesemanii</i> has evolved a higher UV-B radiation tolerance than its close relative, <i>A. thaliana</i>. Two accessions of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> were obtained from locations of relatively close proximity to each other. <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston was collected just west of Kingston, New Zealand, at an altitude of ~â€‰500â€‰m and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek was collected 20â€‰km north of Kingston at an altitude of ~â€‰300â€‰m. The <i>P. cheesemanii</i> phenotypes were compared against those of the widely studied <i>A. thaliana</i> accession Col-0, which grows at an altitude of up to 100â€‰m (<a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">www.arabidopsis.org</a>), and the UV-B-resistant accession Kondara (distribution altitude: 1000â€“1100â€‰m) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Gegas VC, Wargent JJ, Pesquet E, Granqvist E, Paul ND, Doonan JH. Endopolyploidy as a potential alternative adaptive strategy for Arabidopsis leaf size variation in response to UV-B. J Exp Bot. 2014;65:2757â€“66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1908">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="SÃ¡nchez-Bermejo E, Castrillo G, Del Llano B, Navarro C, Zarco-FernÃ¡ndez S, Martinez-Herrera DJ, Leo-del Puerto Y, MuÃ±oz R, CÃ¡mara C, Paz-Ares J. Natural variation in arsenate tolerance identifies an arsenate reductase in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nat Commun. 2014;5:4617." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d63571e1911">32</a>]. To test for responses to UV-B radiation, 28-day-old <i>A. thaliana</i> plants and 38-day-old <i>P. cheesemanii</i> plants, of similar plant size, were treated with UV-B radiation for 5 days to allow the manifestation of typical UV-B radiation phenotypic responses. A moderately high UV-B radiation (5.2â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup>) was used to induce both UVR8-dependent and -independent responses.</p><p>Leaves of UV-B radiation-treated <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 and Kondara plants were significantly smaller than leaves from untreated controls, and the Col-0 accession displayed more necrotic lesions on its leaves than Kondara (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig3">3</a>a, b, e, f, i, j and Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>a). <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek plants showed a smaller but significant decrease in leaf size upon UV-B radiation compared to untreated controls. Interestingly, the leaf size of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston was not affected by UV-B radiation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>a). All plants displayed some leaf curling and the leaves attained a glossy appearance, which was most apparent in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig3">3</a>c, d, g, h, k, l).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="509" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Twenty-eight-day-old <i>A. thaliana</i> and 38-day-old <i>P. cheesemanii</i> plants after a 5-day UV-B treatment. <i>A. thaliana</i> (28â€‰days old) and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (38â€‰days old) plants were grown in long day conditions and subsequently transferred to UV-B-supplemented white light for 5â€‰days (UV-B-5-day) or to white light only (control). <b>a</b> <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 <b>b</b> <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara <b>c</b> <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston <b>d</b> <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek plants grown under control conditions. <b>e</b> <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 <b>f</b> <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara <b>g</b> <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston <b>h</b> <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek plants after UV-B treatment. <b>i</b>-<b>l</b> Enlarged insets are shown for UV-B-treated plants (e-h) only. Arrows indicate necrotic lesions (white), leaf curling (green) and glossy appearance (yellow), respectively. Scale bars, 3.5â€‰cm</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="277" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Total chlorophyll content and leaf size of <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> plants grown with and without UV-B radiation. <i>A. thaliana</i> (28â€‰days old) and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> (38â€‰days old) plants were grown in long day conditions and subsequently transferred to UV-B-supplemented white light for 5â€‰days (UV-B-5-day) or to white light only (control). <b>a</b> Total leaf area. <b>b</b> Total leaf chlorophyll content. 1, <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0; 2, <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara; 3, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston; 4, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek. Error bars represent SEM (Studentâ€™s <i>t</i>-test; *, <i>p</i>â€‰&lt;â€‰0.05; **, <i>p</i>â€‰&lt;â€‰0.01). Data were collected from 4 to 8 biological replicates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, we determined chlorophyll concentration in fully mature leaves of the different accessions. A significant increase in chlorophyll concentration was found in leaves of UV-B radiation-treated <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston plants, compared to untreated controls, while chlorophyll concentration did not change in <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>b).</p><p>Taken together, our results support the notion that <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions exhibit a higher UV-B radiation tolerance than the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions. Moreover, the two <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions responded to UV-B radiation in different ways.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Distinct expression of UV-B radiation-inducible genes in <i>Pachycladon cheesemanii</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>
</h3><p>To further examine the UV-B radiation responses in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, we identified the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> homologues of 11 <i>A. thaliana</i> genes that function in the UVR8-dependent pathway and three homologues that play a role in the UVR8-independent pathway. The protein sequences of these genes were used to search the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome draft using TBLASTN. As a result, at least two potential copies of each gene were identified (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM8">8</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM9">9</a>), consistent with the polyploid nature of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome. Primers for the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genes were designed to amplify conserved protein-coding regions, such that both copies were expected to be amplified with equal efficiency.</p><p><i>P. cheesemanii</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> plants were treated with UV-B radiation for 5 h to focus on early transcriptional effects and limit secondary responses. Gene expression of the selected genes was measured by quantitative real-time polymerase chain reaction (RT-qPCR). We initially measured 11 genes induced in <i>A. thaliana</i> by the UVR8-dependent pathway and found that eight (<i>HY5</i>, <i>HYH</i>, <i>CHS</i>, <i>ELIP1</i>, <i>CRYPTOCHROME 3</i> (<i>CRY3</i>), <i>GLUTATHIONE PEROXIDASE 7</i> (<i>GPX7</i>), <i>SIGMA FACTOR 5</i> (<i>SIG5</i>), and <i>WALL-ASSOCIATED RECEPTOR KINASE-LIKE 8</i> (<i>WAKL8</i>)) were upregulated by UV-B radiation in both <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions and three were not (<i>BCB</i>, a gene encoding a blue copper binding protein, <i>COP1</i>, and <i>GEM-RELATED 5</i> (<i>GER5</i>), which encodes a protein involved in hormone-mediated regulation of seed germination). Interestingly, while most of these genes were also upregulated in both <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions, the extent of upregulation was generally lower (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig5">5</a>).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="572" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Relative expression of genes in UVR8-dependent and UVR8-independent UV-B response pathways in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. Six-week-old <i>A. thaliana</i> and nine-week-old <i>P. cheesemanii</i> plants were treated with white light (Control) or white light supplemented with UV-B radiation for 5 h (UV-B-5â€‰h). Mature leaves were harvested for RT-qPCR analysis. The expression of transcripts indicated in each of the graphs was normalized to the reference genes of <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i>, respectively. Relative expression (fold change) was calculated by dividing expression values of UV-B-treated plants with those of control-treated plants. 1, <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0; 2, <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara; 3, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston; 4, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek. Data were from three independent biological replicates. Error bars represent SEM. Means with different lowercase letters indicate values of UV-B treated samples that are significantly different between the accessions analyzed (Tukeyâ€™s HSD, <i>p</i>â€‰&lt;â€‰0.05)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next quantified three genes of the UVR8-independent pathway, i.e., genes encoding <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> WRKY DNA-BINDING PROTEIN 30 (<i>WRKY30</i>), URIDINE DIPHOSPHATE GLYCOSYLTRANSFERASE 74E2 (<i>UGT74E2</i>), and FAD-LINKED OXIDOREDUCTASE (<i>FOX1</i>), and none of those was induced significantly in the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions by 5.2â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> of UV. However, the <i>WRKY30</i> homologue was upregulated in both <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions and the transcript levels of <i>UGT74E2</i> and <i>FOX1</i> were elevated in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek, but not in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston. Thus, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions responded in different ways to UV-B radiation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Similar UV-B radiation-repair systems in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>
</h3><p>Plants reduce susceptibility to UV radiation-induced damage through photorepair and dark repair systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gill SS, Anjum NA, Gill R, Jha M, Tuteja N. DNA damage and repair in plants under ultraviolet and ionizing radiations. Sci World J. 2015;2015:250158." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2330">33</a>]. Here, we identified <i>P. cheesemanii</i> homologues of six key genes involved in UV-B radiation-repair systems in <i>A. thaliana</i>. The UV-B radiation-induced transcript level of each gene was subsequently measured in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> by RT-qPCR. In response to UV-B radiation, the two photorepair genes <i>PHOTOLYASE 1</i> (<i>PHR1</i>) and <i>UV REPAIR DEFECTIVE 3</i> (<i>UVR3</i>) were significantly upregulated in all four plant accessions, with <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara showing the highest increase (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig6">6</a>). Interestingly, four genes involved in dark repair (nucleotide excision repair) or representing the <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologue of <i>Xeroderma Pigmentosum Complementation Group B1</i> (<i>XPB1</i>) from humans, i.e., <i>Arabidopsis thaliana XERODERMA PIGMENTOSUM GROUP D</i> (<i>XPD</i>), and a homologue of the human <i>ERCC1</i> gene (<i>ERCC1</i>), did not show obvious UV-B-induced transcript level changes in <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 or the two <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions, while <i>XPD</i> and <i>ERCC1</i> were upregulated 3.5-fold in <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig6">6</a>). Thus, the photorepair genes were upregulated in all plant accessions, while only <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara showed an activated dark repair system.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="766" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Relative expression of genes involved in DNA damage repair in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. Two genes involved in photorepair (<i>PHR1</i> and <i>UVR3</i>) and four genes involved in nucleotide excision repair were selected. Six-week-old <i>A. thaliana</i> and nine-week-old <i>P. cheesemanii</i> plants were treated with white light (Control) or white light supplemented with UV-B radiation for 5 h (UV-B-5â€‰h). Mature leaves were harvested for RT-qPCR analysis. The expression of transcripts indicated in each of the graphs was normalized to the reference genes of <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i>, respectively. Relative expression (fold change) was calculated by dividing expression values of UV-B-treated plants with thoes of control-treated plants. Data were from three independent biological replicates. 1, <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0; 2, <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara; 3, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston; 4, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Wye creek. Error bars represent SEM. Means with different lowercase letters indicate values of UV-B treated samples that are significantly different between the accessions analyzed (Tukeyâ€™s HSD, <i>p</i>â€‰&lt;â€‰0.05)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10"><i>Pachycladon cheesemanii</i> may originate from different Brassicaceae lineages</h3><p>The two <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenomes have been proposed to result from a relatively young hybridization event, between unknown species, that occurred in New Zealand about 1.61 million years ago [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2477">5</a>]. Here, we sequenced the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome and used it to help trace back the origin of the subgenomes of <i>Pachycladon</i> and to analyze UV-B radiation responses.</p><p>In our synteny analysis of 28 available Brassicaceae genomes, all eight species from tribes Boechereae and Camelineae (genera <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Camelina</i>, <i>Capsella</i> and <i>Boechera</i>) displayed consistent high cumulative genome alignment percentages, regardless as to which of the eight species was combined with any of the 27 other species. Values for Boecherea (<i>B. stricta</i>) were the highest, suggesting that at least one of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> subgenomes originated from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig1">1</a>a). Nevertheless, no cumulative values higher than 50% were found, suggesting that only one of the <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenomes has the same origin as these available Brassicaceae genomes. The findings are in general agreement with a recent Brassicaceae phylogenetic tree, established using 113 nuclear genes as markers, which proposes that <i>Pachycladon</i> is closely related to species of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Camelina</i>, <i>Capsella</i> and <i>Boechera</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2533">29</a>]. Similarly, Joly et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2536">5</a>] reported that one of the predicted ancestral subgenomes of <i>Pachycladon</i> has close phylogenetic relationships with <i>Boechera</i> and <i>Capsella</i> from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage. However, the origin of the other subgenome is still unclear although phylogenetic analysis of the chloroplast <i>rbcL</i> gene in all Brassicaceae species suggested this genome to be the maternal ancestral subgenome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2555">5</a>]. Heenan et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Heenan PB, Goeke DF, Houliston GJ, Lysak MA. Phylogenetic analyses of ITS and rbcL DNA sequences for sixteen genera of Australian and New Zealand Brassicaceae result in the expansion of the tribe Microlepidieae. Taxon. 2012;61:970â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2558">34</a>] proposed that the limited information on Brassicaceae species genomes hampered efforts to determine the origin of the second subgenome, and it may have originated from the tribe Smelowskieae or its close relatives that have subsequently gone extinct [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Heenan PB, Goeke DF, Houliston GJ, Lysak MA. Phylogenetic analyses of ITS and rbcL DNA sequences for sixteen genera of Australian and New Zealand Brassicaceae result in the expansion of the tribe Microlepidieae. Taxon. 2012;61:970â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2562">34</a>]. In contrast, we found that the second subgenome might have originated from Brassicaceae Lineage EII. The highest cumulative values were generated when using the combination of <i>B. stricta</i> and <i>E. heterophyllum</i>, the latter of which is from Brassicaceae Lineage EII [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2571">29</a>]. The Eutrema tribe was predicted to be phylogenetically further away from <i>Pachycladon</i> than the Boechera tribe, however, it is close to the Brassiceae tribe phylogenetically, which could explain the <i>Brassica</i> trace in the <i>Pachycladon</i> genome found previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Joly S, Heenan PB, Lockhart PJ. A Pleistocene inter-tribal allopolyploidization event precedes the species radiation of Pachycladon (Brassicaceae) in New Zealand. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2009;51:365â€“72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2584">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Huang C-H, Sun R, Hu Y, Zeng L, Zhang N, Cai L, Zhang Q, Koch MA, Al-Shehbaz I, Edger PP. Resolution of Brassicaceae phylogeny using nuclear genes uncovers nested radiations and supports convergent morphological evolution. Mol Biol Evol. 2015;33:394â€“412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2587">29</a>]. Unfortunately, currently little genome information is available for Brassicaceae Lineage EII species, which limits the confident identification of the second <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenome ancestor. Altogether, we concluded that one of <i>Pachycladon</i> subgenomes has an origin similar to that of <i>B. stricta</i>, and that the other possibly arose from Brassicaceae Lineage EII.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Distinct UV-B radiation tolerance responses in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>P. cheesemanii</i> species</h3><p>Earlier research has reported that UV-B radiation-tolerant plants retained relatively high levels of chlorophyll after UV-B exposure [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Greenberg BM, Wilson MI, Huang X-D, Duxbury CL, Gerhardt KE, Gensemer RW. The effects of ultraviolet-B radiation on higher plants. In: Gorsuch JW, Hughes JS, editors. Wuncheng W Wang, Plants for environmental studies. Florida: CRC Press; 1997. p. 1â€“35." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2614">35</a>]. Consistent with this, a higher chlorophyll concentration was found in UV-B radiation-tolerant <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara as compared to the less UV-B radiation-tolerant accession <i>A. thaliana</i> Col-0 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>b) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Gegas VC, Wargent JJ, Pesquet E, Granqvist E, Paul ND, Doonan JH. Endopolyploidy as a potential alternative adaptive strategy for Arabidopsis leaf size variation in response to UV-B. J Exp Bot. 2014;65:2757â€“66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2626">31</a>]. The UV-B response is regulated by at least two pathways of which one is UVR8-dependent while the other does not require UVR8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li N, Teranishi M, Yamaguchi H, Matsushita T, Watahiki MK, Tsuge T, Li S-S, Hidema J. UV-B-induced CPD photolyase gene expression is regulated by UVR8-dependent and-independent pathways in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Physiol. 2015;56:2014â€“23." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2630">36</a>]. The UVR8-dependent pathway induces the expression of six genes (<i>CHS</i>, <i>ELIP1</i>, <i>CRY3</i>, <i>GPX7</i>, <i>SIG5</i>, <i>WAKL8</i>) which is mediated by the transcription factors HY5 and HYH [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Brown BA, Jenkins GI. UV-B signaling pathways with different fluence-rate response profiles are distinguished in mature Arabidopsis leaf tissue by requirement for UVR8, HY5, and HYH. Plant Physiol. 2008;146:576â€“88." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2652">26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Jiang L, Wang Y, BjÃ¶rn LO, Li S. Arabidopsis radical-induced cell death1 is involved in UV-B signaling. Photoch Photobio Sci. 2009;8:838â€“46." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2655">37</a>]. Here we found that the genes encoding the transcription factors HY5 and HYH and the six above-mentioned genes were induced in response to the UV-B radiation, suggesting that the UVR8-dependent pathway was upregulated in both <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara and Col-0 accessions. Of note, however, gene expression was generally more induced in <i>A. thaliana</i> Kondara than in Col-0 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig5">5</a>). In addition, accession Kondara showed a greater induction of genes involved in photorepair and nucleotide excision repair (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig6">6</a>), which may allow it to recover from, or prevent UV-B-induced radiation stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="MuÃ±oz MJ, Moreno NN, Giono LE, Botto AEC, Dujardin G, Bastianello G, Lavore S, Torres-MÃ©ndez A, Menck CF, Blencowe BJ. Major roles for pyrimidine dimers, nucleotide excision repair, and ATR in the alternative splicing response to UV irradiation. Cell Rep. 2017;18:2868â€“79." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2671">38</a>]. Thus, the induction of chlorophyll biosynthesis genes and a stronger activation of HY5/HYH-pathway genes in Kondara than in Col-0, along with an increased DNA repair capacity in Kondara could be main contributors to its UV-B radiation tolerance.</p><p>Remarkably, leaf growth of the two <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions studied here was less affected by UV-B radiation than that of the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions Kondara and Col-0; <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston did not show a significant reduction of leaf area upon exposure to UV-B radiation compared to plants grown at control conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>a). We found that the basal chlorophyll levels in the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions were higher than those of the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions. Furthermore, chlorophyll concentration in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston further elevated upon UV-B radiation and this correlated with its increased tolerance. Chlorophyll levels have been suggested to be an indicator of UV-B radiation tolerance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Greenberg BM, Wilson MI, Huang X-D, Duxbury CL, Gerhardt KE, Gensemer RW. The effects of ultraviolet-B radiation on higher plants. In: Gorsuch JW, Hughes JS, editors. Wuncheng W Wang, Plants for environmental studies. Florida: CRC Press; 1997. p. 1â€“35." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2699">35</a>] and the observed correlation between UV-B radiation tolerance, chlorophyll levels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#Fig4">4</a>b), and the ability to further increase chlorophyll levels upon UV-B radiation is consistent with that suggestion. The <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions generally induced genes of the UVR8-dependent pathway to a lower extent than the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions, possibly as a result of a higher basal tolerance to UV-B radiation. Brown &amp; Jenkins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Brown BA, Jenkins GI.
