UV-B signaling pathways with different fluence-rate response profiles are distinguished in mature Arabidopsis leaf tissue by requirement for UVR8, HY5, and HYH. Plant Physiol. 2008;146:576â€“88." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2712">26</a>] identified three genes (<i>WRKY30</i>, <i>FOX1</i> and <i>UGT74E2</i>) in <i>A. thaliana</i> that were induced in response to 3â€“12â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> UV-B radiation as part of the still poorly characterized UVR8-independent pathway. However, in contrast to the <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions, both <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions showed strong induction of the <i>WRKY30</i> gene at moderately high UV-B radiation levels of 5.2â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup>. Crucially, only the Wye creek accession of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> induced expression of the two other genes (<i>FOX1</i> and <i>UGT74E2</i>), suggesting that the UVR8-independent pathway may be more important in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> than in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Thus, we found a positive correlation between chlorophyll content and UV-B radiation tolerance, and while the stronger induction of the UVR8-dependent pathway in <i>A. thaliana</i> accession Kondara may be at the basis of its UV-B radiation tolerance, the UVR8-independent pathway may play a more prominent role in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions. Of further interest is that the lower UV-B radiation tolerance of the Wye creek accession correlated with a much stronger upregulation of genes involved in the UVR8-independent pathway, further supporting that this pathway plays an important role in coping with UV-B radiation damage in <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. Indeed, <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions may be particularly well suited for further exploring the UVR8-independent UV-B response pathway helping to increase our understanding of the genomic and genetic basis of plant adaptations to environmental UV-B stress.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>The polyploid nature of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome may have contributed to its relatively high tolerance to UV-B radiation. We suggest that tolerance is achieved through the activation of the UVR8-independent response pathway. Our results are important for understanding the allopolyploidy nature of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome and may be used to improve UV-B stress tolerance in Brassicaceae crop plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Plant growth and UV-B treatment</h3><p>Seeds of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston (geographical coordinates in decimal degrees âˆ’â€‰45.3273, 168.7078) and Wye creek (geographical coordinates in decimal degrees âˆ’â€‰45.1398, 168.7672) were provided by Dr. Claudia Voelckel (Max Planck Institute for Chemical Ecology, Jena, Germany) and Dr. Peter Heenan (Wildland Consultants Ltd., Rotorua, New Zealand). Seeds of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (L.) Heynh. accessions Col-0 and Kondara were obtained from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Biological Resource Center (<i>ABRC</i>; <a href="https://abrc.osu.edu">https://abrc.osu.edu</a>). Seeds of both species were sown and germinated in wet Seed Raising MixÂ® soil from Daltons (Matamata, New Zealand) and seedlings were grown under a 16-h light (200â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> cool-white fluorescent tube)/8-h dark (long-day) regime at 22â€‰Â°C. For UV-B treatment, the two <i>P. cheesemanii</i> accessions were sown 10â€‰days earlier than the <i>A. thaliana</i> plants to achieve the same plant size. After 28 (<i>A. thaliana</i>) or 38 (<i>P. cheesemanii</i>) days of growth, the plants were transferred to the UV-B radiation chamber where they were subjected to normal white light (200â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> cool-white fluorescent tube) supplemented with 5.2â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> UV-B (290â€“320â€‰nm) for UV-B treatments, while the control plants were kept under white light conditions. The UV-B fluorescent tubes used in the chamber were Q-Panel 313 (Q-Lab Corp, Cleveland, OH, USA), which were wrapped in 0.13-mm-thick cellulose diacetate foil (Clarifoil; Courtaulds Ltd., Derby, UK) to remove wavelengths &lt;â€‰290â€‰nm. The chamber was split into a UV-B+ zone and a UV-Bâˆ’ zone separated by a central curtain of UV-B opaque film (Lumivar; BPI Visqueen, Ardeer, UK). For the UV-Bâˆ’ zone, the UV-B tubes were wrapped in the same UV-B opaque film [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wargent J, Nelson B, McGhie T, Barnes P. Acclimation to UV-B radiation and visible light in Lactuca sativa involves up-regulation of photosynthetic performance and orchestration of metabolome-wide responses. Plant Cell Environ. 2015;38:929â€“40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2843">39</a>]. UV-B treatments were quantified at plant canopy height with an Optronics OL-756 UV-VIS Spectroradiometer (Optronic Laboratories, Gooch and Housego, FL, USA) equipped with integrating sphere. Spectroradiometric scans of the controlled environment chamber confirmed that the biologically effective UV dose was &lt;â€‰0.01â€‰kJâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup> d<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> in the UV-Bâˆ’ zone. UV-B-treated and nontreated plants were collected 5â€‰days after the treatment. For quantification of transcript abundance, seedlings were grown under a 10-h light (200â€‰Î¼molâ€‰m<sup>âˆ’â€‰2</sup>â€‰s<sup>âˆ’â€‰1</sup> cool-white fluorescent lamp)/14-h dark (short-day) regime at 22â€‰Â°C for 6 weeks (<i>A. thaliana</i>) and 9 weeks (<i>P. cheesemanii</i>) to obtain a large leaf area to maximise UV-B radiation absorption. The plants were subsequently mock treated or UV-B irradiated for 5 hours, after which leaf tissue was collected.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Library preparation and Illumina sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was prepared from <i>P. cheesemanii</i> Kingston leaves. DNA was extracted using a modified CTAB DNA extraction protocol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gawel N, Jarret R. A modified CTAB DNA extraction procedure for Musa and Ipomoea. Plant Mol Biol Rep. 1991;9:262â€“6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2872">40</a>] with extra steps to remove chloroplast DNA. Purified <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genomic DNA was used to generate 550-bp paired-end (PE) and 1.5-kb to 15-kb mate-pair (MP) sequencing libraries. Following library quality control, the libraries were sequenced on two lanes of an Illumina HiSeq 2500 machine, with 2â€‰Ã—â€‰125 PE and 2â€‰Ã—â€‰125 MP reads generating a total of ~â€‰56 Gb raw sequencing data. This represents a theoretical coverage of 100-fold based on the estimated size of the <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM10">10</a>). PE and MP library construction and Illumina sequencing were performed by New Zealand Genomics Limited (NZGL, Otago, New Zealand).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly and assessment</h3><p>To assess data quality, raw reads were analyzed using SolexaQA++ v3.1.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Cox MP, Peterson DA, Biggs PJ. SolexaQA: at-a-glance quality assessment of Illumina second-generation sequencing data. BMC bioinformatics. 2010;11:485." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2892">41</a>] (Cox et al., 2010). Adaptors were trimmed using trim_galore v0.4.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Krueger F. Trim galore: a wrapper tool around Cutadapt and FastQC to consistently apply quality and adapter trimming to FastQ files. 2015. Available from &#xA;http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/trim_galore/&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2895">42</a>], and the processed reads were used for assembly. SOAPdenovo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xie Y, Wu G, Tang J, Luo R, Patterson J, Liu S, Huang W, He G, Gu S, Li S. SOAPdenovo-trans: de novo transcriptome assembly with short RNA-Seq reads. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1660â€“6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2898">43</a>] and Platanus v1.2.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kajitani R, Toshimoto K, Noguchi H, Toyoda A, Ogura Y, Okuno M, Yabana M, Harada M, Nagayasu E, Maruyama H. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 2014. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.170720.113&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2901">44</a>] were used for assembly and different k-mer sizes were used to produce multiple assemblies for further quality assessment (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM2">2</a>). Assembly statistics were generated by QUAST v4.1 and Bowtie 2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Gurevich A, Saveliev V, Vyahhi N, Tesler G. QUAST: quality assessment tool for genome assemblies. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:1072â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2908">45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL. Fast gapped-read alignment with bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012;9(4):357." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2911">46</a>]. Leaf transcriptome data (full transcripts) of <i>P. cheesemanii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Gruenheit N, Deusch O, Esser C, Becker M, Voelckel C, Lockhart P. Cutoffs and k-mers: implications from a transcriptome study in allopolyploid plants. BMC Genomics. 2012;13:92." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2917">6</a>] (downloaded from NCBI) were used for mapping to the genome assemblies using PASA v2.0.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith RK Jr, Hannick LI, Maiti R, Ronning CM, Rusch DB, Town CD. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003;31:5654â€“66." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2920">47</a>]. BUSCO v3.0.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="SimÃ£o FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210â€“2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2923">48</a>] (dataset: â€œembryophyta_odb9â€ containing 1440 orthogroups, downloaded from <a href="http://busco.ezlab.org">http://busco.ezlab.org</a>) was used to evaluate the completeness of the assemblies. The result metrics from the tools mentioned above were used to select the best assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM2">2</a>). Samtools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li H, Handsaker B, Wysoker A, Fennell T, Ruan J, Homer N, Marth G, Abecasis G, Durbin R. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:2078â€“9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2934">49</a>] was used for identifying SNPs. After quality filtering, the approximate genome size was determined using the total base number of sequencing data and sequencing depth being derived from the k-mer distribution using the formula [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhang G, Liu X, Quan Z, Cheng S, Xu X, Pan S, Xie M, Zeng P, Yue Z, Wang W. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat Biotechnol. 2012;30:549â€“54." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2937">50</a>]: Gâ€‰=â€‰(NÃ—(L-Kâ€‰+â€‰1)-B)/D; G, genome size; N, number of reads; L, length of reads; K, length of k-mer; B, low-frequency k-mers with occurrence less than four times; D, sequencing depth corresponding to selected k-mer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Identification of repeats</h3><p>RepeatModeler v1.0.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Smit A, Hubley R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0.8. 2008. Available from &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2948">51</a>], employing RepeatScout, Tandem Repeats Finder and RECON modules, was used to construct a <i>de novo</i> repeats library for <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. LTR_finder v1.0.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Xu Z, Wang H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(suppl_2):W265â€“8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2957">52</a>], TransposonPSI v08222010 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Haas B. TransposonPSI: an application of PSI-blast to mine (retro-) transposon ORF homologies. 2007. Available from &#xA;http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net/&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2960">53</a>], and MITE-hunter v11â€“2011 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Han Y, Wessler SR. MITE-hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 2010;38:e199." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2964">54</a>] were used to identify Long Terminal Repeats (LTRs), retrotransposons, and Miniature Inverted repeat Transposable Elements (MITEs), respectively. Viridiplantae repeats were extracted from Repbase repeat libraries [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kapitonov VV, Jurka J. A universal classification of eukaryotic transposable elements implemented in Repbase. Nat Rev Genet. 2008;9:411â€“2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2967">55</a>]. The assembly was masked by <i>de novo</i> and Viridiplantae repeats using RepeatMasker v4.0.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0.5. 2015. Available from &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2973">56</a>] to estimate the non-redundant genomic repeat content. The repeats were further classified using the RepeatClassifier module of RepeatModeler.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Functional annotation</h3><p>A combination of <i>ab initio</i> and homology-based annotation methods was used in the Maker pipeline v2.31.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Campbell MS, Holt C, Moore B, Yandell M. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKER-P. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2014;48:4.11.11â€“14.11.39." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2988">57</a>] for gene prediction. Briefly, AUGUSTUS v3.2.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Stanke M, Diekhans M, Baertsch R, Haussler D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics. 2008;24:637â€“44." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2991">58</a>] was used with an <i>Arabidopsis</i> training set and leaf transcriptome alignments from PASA were used to improve and provide evidence for gene prediction. Gene annotation was done using BLASTP v2.6.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ. Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990;215:403â€“10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d63571e2997">59</a>] and BLASTX against Uniprot (Swissprot + TrEMBL) and the NCBI nr database (only BLASTX), restricting the search to Viridiplantae using taxonomy filter, best hit, E-value cutoff of 1e-20, query coverage of â‰¥50% and percentage identity â‰¥50%. Annotations for each gene were selected as per the following order of preferences: 1) Swissprot (BLASTP), 2) Swissprot (BLASTX), 3) Uniprot (BLASTP), 4) Uniprot (BLASTX), 5) NCBI-nr (BLASTX). InterProScan v5.22â€“61.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Jones P, Binns D, Chang H-Y, Fraser M, Li W, McAnulla C, McWilliam H, Maslen J, Mitchell A, Nuka G. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1236â€“40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3001">60</a>] was used for function annotation of the protein domains and families using all available databases in InterProScan. GO annotations were obtained from Uniprot database. Transcription factors were predicted using PlantTFDB v4.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Jin J, Tian F, Yang D-C, Meng Y-Q, Kong L, Luo J, Gao G. PlantTFDB 4.0: toward a central hub for transcription factors and regulatory interactions in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 2017;45:D1040â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3004">61</a>]. KAAS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Moriya Y, Itoh M, Okuda S, Yoshizawa AC, Kanehisa M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35(suppl_2):W182â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3007">62</a>] was used to obtain KEGG pathway annotations. MISA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Thiel T, Michalek W, Varshney R, Graner A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor Appl Genet. 2003;106:411â€“22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3010">63</a>] was used to predict SSR markers. Infernal v1.1.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Nawrocki EP, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:2933â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3013">64</a>] was used to predict non-coding RNAs with an E-value cutoff of 1e-5 and removing low-scoring overlaps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Comparative genomics</h3><p>Genomes of available Brassicaceae species were downloaded from NCBI and Phytozome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Goodstein DM, Shu S, Howson R, Neupane R, Hayes RD, Fazo J, Mitros T, Dirks W, Hellsten U, Putnam N. Phytozome. A comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 2011;40(D1):D1178â€“86." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3024">65</a>]. Nucmer from MUMmer v3.23 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Delcher AL, Salzberg SL, Phillippy AM. Using MUMmer to identify similar regions in large sequence sets. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2003; 00:10.3.1-18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3027">66</a>] was used to align <i>P. cheesemanii</i> genome sequences against each Brassicaceae genome to perform synteny analysis. Delta filter was applied to select only one-to-one alignments. OrthoFinder v2.1.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Emms DM, Kelly S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 2015;16:157." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3033">67</a>] was used to find orthologs among the protein sequences of the selected genomes. GO annotations for <i>A. thaliana</i> were obtained from Araport11 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Cheng CY, Krishnakumar V, Chan AP, Thibaud-Nissen F, Schobel S, Town CD. Araport11 a complete reannotation of the Arabidopsis thaliana reference genome. Plant J. 2017;9:789â€“804." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3040">68</a>], and for <i>B. stricta</i> and <i>C. sativa</i>, the GO annotations were obtained in the same way as for <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. The dendrogram was plotted using iTOL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Letunic I, Bork P. Interactive tree of life (iTOL) v3. an online tool for the display and annotation of phylogenetic and other trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44(W1):W242â€“5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3052">69</a>]. Overlaps between orthogroups were plotted using the ClusterVenn utility in OrthoVenn [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wang Y, Coleman-Derr D, Chen G, Gu YQ. OrthoVenn: a web server for genome wide comparison and annotation of orthologous clusters across multiple species. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43(W1):W78â€“84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3055">70</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Chlorophyll content measurement</h3><p>Chlorophyll content was measured by using a modified chlorophyll analysis protocol based on Ignat et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ignat T, Schmilovitch Z, FefÃ¶ldi J, Bernstein N, Steiner B, Egozi H, Hoffman A. Nonlinear methods for estimation of maturity stage, total chlorophyll, and carotenoid content in intact bell peppers. Biosyst Eng. 2013;114:414â€“25." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3066">71</a>]. Two fresh mature leaves were immersed in 10â€‰ml 95% ethanol, and tubes were placed in a cool room (4â€‰Â°C) for 24â€‰h. The chlorophyll-extraction solution was then collected into new tubes, and a further 10â€‰ml ethanol was added to the leaves for another 4â€‰h to collect the remainder of the chlorophyll. The combined 20â€‰ml chlorophyll-extraction solution was mixed well and 2â€‰ml was used to quantify the chlorophyll content at 664 and 649â€‰nm by using the following formulas [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ignat T, Schmilovitch Z, FefÃ¶ldi J, Bernstein N, Steiner B, Egozi H, Hoffman A. Nonlinear methods for estimation of maturity stage, total chlorophyll, and carotenoid content in intact bell peppers. Biosyst Eng. 2013;114:414â€“25." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3069">71</a>]:
</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$ \mathrm{Chlorophyll}\ a=\left(13.36\times A664-5.19\times A649\right) $$</span></div></div>
<div id="Equb" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$ \mathrm{Chlorophyll}\ b=\left(27.43\times A649-8.12\times A664\right) $$</span></div></div>
<div id="Equc" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$ \mathrm{Total}\ \mathrm{chlorophyll}=\left(5.24\times A664+22.24\times A649\right) $$</span></div></div><p>Subsequently, chlorophyll contents were normalized to the corresponding total leaf areas that were used to extract chlorophyll.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">RNA extraction, cDNA synthesis and RT-qPCR</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from mature leaves with a Quick-RNA MiniPrep Kit (Zymo Research, CA, USA) and treated with DNase I to remove genomic DNA contamination. Reverse transcription was performed with an oligo (dT) primer using a Transcriptor First Strand cDNA Synthesis Kit (Roche, Basel, Switzerland). The gene sequences and primers used in RT-qPCR are listed in Additional files <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM9">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#MOESM11">11</a>. RT-qPCR was performed using a LightCycler 480 SYBR Green I Master kit (Roche) (LightCycler 480; Roche). For real-time PCR quantification, <i>AT4G29130</i> was used as a reference gene in <i>A. thaliana</i>, and the homologue of <i>AT4G34270</i> was used as a reference gene in <i>P. cheesemanii</i> based on previous reports [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Castells E, Molinier J, Drevensek S, Genschik P, Barneche F, Bowler C. det1-1-induced UV-C hyposensitivity through UVR3 and PHR1 photolyase gene over-expression. Plant J. 2010;63:392â€“404." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3168">72</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Castelain M, Le Hir R, Bellini C. The non-DNA-binding bHLH transcription factor PRE3/bHLH135/ATBS1/TMO7 is involved in the regulation of light signaling pathway in Arabidopsis. Physiol Plantarum. 2012;145:450â€“60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-6084-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d63571e3171">73</a>]. The <i>P. cheesemanii</i> homologue of <i>AT4G34270</i> was identified by applying TBLASTN.</p></div></div></section>

                  </dd><dt class="c-abbreviation_list__term"><dfn>XPD:</dfn></dt><dd class="c-abbreviation_list__description">

                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM1"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-6084-4/MediaObjects/12864_2019_6084_MOESM1_ESM.docx" data-supp-info-image=""><b>Additional file 1.</b></a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p> K-mer analysis for estimating the genome size of <i>P. cheesemanii</i>. The genome size was estimated by using the formula: Gâ€‰=â€‰(N x(L-Kâ€‰+â€‰1)-B)/D. G, genome size; N, number of reads; L, length of reads; K, length of k-mer; B, low-frequency k-mers with occurrence less than four times; D, coverage depth corresponding to selected k-mer. 41,51,61,71,81,91, and 101-mer sizes were analyzed, and the coverage depth of 41-mer was selected for genome size estimation.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item"
